  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a91de084-9fd6-41dc-95c8-7e3a19e9c6fb'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">By and large, it did not seem so to me. There were one or two things edging towards corn, particularly towards the end. Most Victorian novels are some<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">By and large, it did not seem so to me. There were one or two things edging towards corn, particularly towards the end. Most Victorian novels are somewhat melodramatic. To me melodramas are things like bad soaps. A man makes his sister-in-law pregnant and the matriarch of the family banishes him from the family.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/5885436-geraldine-o-hagan"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1351847412i/5885436._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/5885436-geraldine-o-hagan">Geraldine O&#39;Hagan</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Interesting question. I’m not sure that he would have. He seemed to need quite a big push to trigger him to action. Then again, he seemed to keep retu<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Interesting question. I’m not sure that he would have. He seemed to need quite a big push to trigger him to action. Then again, he seemed to keep returning to the moment that George read the obituary as the key moment for him in terms of his hatred for Lady Audley, rather than the murder itself. Still, overall I think that if George was alive, Robert would have followed his lead, and it seemed that George would not have pursued the issue. Also, Robert’s ‘investigation’ was entirely based around tracing the movements of George, Lucy and Helen and trying to connect them. His end goal was to link them and find George. Without that mystery to solve his involvement would only have been as a sympathetic friend to George, and a concerned relative. So I think the only question would be whether or not he would inform Lord Audley, or let him live in ignorance. And on that point I think he would awkwardly defer to George, unless there was a prospect of a technically illegitimate baby who would effect Alicia’s inheritance. An issue which for some reason is never raised in the novel. Is there some reason that there is no chance of an heir, which is known to the family? Otherwise it would seem to be a potential complication. Lady Audley could have used a claimed pregnancy as a way to defer her imprisonment, but it seems to be the one thing that she never thinks to try.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15214828646832283692">whatever could be Lady Audley's secret? could it be... <i>murder</i>? miscegenation? malfeasance? misdirected malevolence ending in tears, tragedy, and general tawdriness? an assumed identity? flatulence? that not-so-fresh feeling? bigamy? bigotry? child abuse? child abandonment? une affaire de coeur? une affaire de blanchiment d'argent? well, all or some of those things may or may not be a part of this novel - but they are not the secret in question. Lady Audley's terrible, terrible secret is... <br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view</a></span>
  <span id="freeText15214828646832283692" style="display:none">whatever could be Lady Audley's secret? could it be... <i>murder</i>? miscegenation? malfeasance? misdirected malevolence ending in tears, tragedy, and general tawdriness? an assumed identity? flatulence? that not-so-fresh feeling? bigamy? bigotry? child abuse? child abandonment? une affaire de coeur? une affaire de blanchiment d'argent? well, all or some of those things may or may not be a part of this novel - but they are not the secret in question. Lady Audley's terrible, terrible secret is... <br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ha! did you actually think i was going to tell you? think again, sucker! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>this ripping victorian yarn is a tale of deceit and possible death, of class barriers breached, a man gone missing, long-held love and quick infatuation, uptight upper class twits mercilessly skewered and drunken lower class buffoons broadly lampooned. it has brilliant and ambitious Lady Audley and her equally ambitious but rather less brilliant servant. it has one of the more charming protagonists i've come across - the lazy, well-meaning, animal-loving young barrister Robert Audley, whose favored activities are smoking his pipe and napping. it has mystery, intrigue, vengeance, and dark rainy nights where evil deeds are afoot. and hey, guess what, it subverts paradigms! sorta.<br><br>Mary Elizabeth Braddon was a popular writer of what appears to be a million potboilers during everyone's favorite english time period, The Victorian Era. nowadays she is practically unknown. that's rather sad because she deserves much better. the novel is stylish and very easy going down. it is the opposite of a torturous experience - it is delightful! and beautifully written as well: a winding but briskly paced narrative with fascinating and often amusing characters, fun twists and turns, overstuffed with lavish description and poetic imagery, and - best of all - an ironically formal and sneakily caustic authorial voice. the wonderful wit in this novel is delicious. delicious! i just used that word to describe a novel! i feel like Oscar Wilde. or Dame Edna.<br><br>if you've read this one, then you know the real selling point: LADY AUDLEY ROCKS! the english class system is no barrier to her dreams. i'd hesitate to call her a feminist icon, but she knows how to make things happen and how to take care of business. such a great villainess. and i barely consider her to be villainous.<br><br><b>
  <i>some spoilers follow</i>
</b>... but i'm still not giving away Lady Audley's secret. mainly because i think it is a rather tedious secret and one of the weaker elements of the novel.<br><br>i am confident that Braddon knew exactly what she was doing when she crafted the character of Lady Audley. this wondrous psychopath utterly rejects her so-called 'place in life' as some drudge in a small town. she uses the weapons she has at her disposal to get ahead: her doll-like beauty, her sweet and cheery smile that lights up a room and makes you feel like you are the most important person in that room, her perfect poise and her perfect manners, her truly phenomenal talent at lying, and her quick-thinking ability to promptly push an annoyingly threatening fellow right down a well. throughout the novel, i was 100% on Lady Audley's team. i love seeing class systems scorned by the underclass and i love seeing them mercilessly dismantled - even if it is a brief and inevitably foiled uprising. Spartacus!<br><br>so yeah, Lady Audley: i love her. her pride, her machinations, her capacity for violence, her elegant skill at avoiding public confrontations with annoying nobodies like resentful stepdaughters, clingy first husbands, and nosy young barristers. oh, Lady Audley, you are the dreamiest of mercenaries!<br><br><a href="http://s181.beta.photobucket.com/user/markmonday/media/ladycannongif_zps0c23ac02.gif.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380366895ra/719279.gif" alt=" photo ladycannongif_zps0c23ac02.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="15214828646832283692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1429335540" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429335540" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1429335540?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199577033" href="/book/show/11700995-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=classics-shelf">classics-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429335540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9114709929387521988"><i>"What do we know of the mysteries that may hang about the houses we enter?"</i><br/><br/>I was both intrigued and hesitant to read this mid-nineteenth century work. First of all, I just had to know this huge secret that Lady Audley would presumably be keeping from us! Secondly, this is labeled as a sensation novel, which to me goes hand in hand with that dreaded term: melodrama. I don’t necessarily mind a bit of verbosity here and there in my classic fiction, in fact I somewhat expect it. However, melodrama i</span>
  <span id="freeText9114709929387521988" style="display:none"><i>"What do we know of the mysteries that may hang about the houses we enter?"</i><br /><br />I was both intrigued and hesitant to read this mid-nineteenth century work. First of all, I just had to know this huge secret that Lady Audley would presumably be keeping from us! Secondly, this is labeled as a sensation novel, which to me goes hand in hand with that dreaded term: melodrama. I don’t necessarily mind a bit of verbosity here and there in my classic fiction, in fact I somewhat expect it. However, melodrama is an element I prefer to steer clear of. I must say I was pleasantly surprised. The taste of melodrama did not turn me off completely. Rather, it only forced me to shave a star from my rating. Otherwise, I quite enjoyed this!<br /><br />I won’t really get into the plot, as it would be too revealing. The author gives away a bit too much up front. However, there is much more to discover farther along, so don’t set it aside too quickly should you choose to pick this one up someday. I relished the gothic atmosphere and the frequent foreshadowing. Audley Court, home to Sir Michael Audley and his much younger second wife, Lady Audley, is an idyllic sort of home but with an aura of mystery about it (much like its inhabitants.)<br /><br /><i>"A noble place; inside as well as out, a noble place—a house in which you incontinently lost yourself if ever you were so rash as to attempt to penetrate its mysteries alone…"</i><br /><br />The characters too are sharply sketched. Robert Audley, barrister and nephew to Sir Michael, is depicted as an idle fellow. One of those types that we would now call ‘book-smart’, but lacking in the area of common sense perhaps. Educated yet aimless. However, when a person dear to his heart turns up missing, the spark is lit and he is jolted into action. He becomes a man obsessed. <br /><br /><i>"The one idea of his life had become his master. He was the bonden slave of one gloomy thought—one horrible presentiment."</i><br /><br />His wits are pitted against those of Lady Audley, a woman who may not be the childlike, helpless young lady she appears to be. The proud yet neglected stepdaughter, Alicia Audley, may be on to my lady, but isn’t she just being a tad bit jealous of all the attention daddy now gives to the new woman in his life? Maybe not; after all, the instincts of animals usually prove to be correct, don’t they?<br /><br /><i>"She may bewitch every man in Essex, but she’d never make friends with my dog."</i><br /><br />I have to admit, I became rather intrigued as the plot moved along towards the second half of the novel. It was quite thrilling, in fact! I may have guessed a bit here and there, but I didn’t quite figure out as much as I expected I would. The end may have been a bit too neat and tidy; the author even admits this to her audience at the end. I’ll gladly accept her apology, as this was a fun romp despite the pat ending. <br /><br /><i>"Foul deeds have been done under the most hospitable roofs; terrible crimes have been committed amid the fairest scenes, and have left no trace upon the spot where they were done."</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9114709929387521988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1418106201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1418106201" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1418106201?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=mystery-crime-and-thrillers">mystery-crime-and-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=3-star-reads">3-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1418106201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15264537771474373340">After around fifty pages or so of reading this I was incredibly disappointed. I’d found out what Lady Audley’s secret was. I didn’t really want to read any further. But, that’s what I was <i>meant</i> to think. Her actual secret isn’t revealed to the very end. And, I must say, I was rather surprised. I didn't see it coming. <br/><br/>It was quite a shocking discovery. I’d spent the rest of the novel is a state of absolute certainty regarding the secret. I thought it was quite a crap secret to be honest. Well, no</span>
  <span id="freeText15264537771474373340" style="display:none">After around fifty pages or so of reading this I was incredibly disappointed. I’d found out what Lady Audley’s secret was. I didn’t really want to read any further. But, that’s what I was <i>meant</i> to think. Her actual secret isn’t revealed to the very end. And, I must say, I was rather surprised. I didn't see it coming. <br><br>It was quite a shocking discovery. I’d spent the rest of the novel is a state of absolute certainty regarding the secret. I thought it was quite a crap secret to be honest. Well, not entirely crap, but revealed too early within the narrative. It disrupted the narrative drive, I felt like I had nothing new to discover. But, again, that’s because it wasn’t the real secret. It was a red herring meant to throw the reader off. The result of this was a pleasant surprise towards the end, though it also meant that the first third of the novel were incredibly slow, whereas the last parts were intense and utterly gripping. Overall, it was a fairly enjoyable book, but I could not get over my lack of engagement with it in the beginning. Its purposeful obviousness made me want to stop reading, but it did make up for it in the end. <br><br><p>
  <b>
    <i> "My intellect is a little way upon the wrong side of that narrow boundary-line between sanity and insanity."</i>
  </b>
</p><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451920071i/17621646._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>This is a sensational novel, so it was written with two purposes. The first was to shock its readership and the second was to make money. It has the obvious shock factor. The fact of what Lady Audley has done is incredibly shocking, and her true secret is even more surprising. To a Victorian audience this would have been scandalous. But, I don’t feel that there’s much beyond that. Yes, the plot had its moments, but literature like this needs more than an exciting plot. When I read a book like this I want to have emotions beyond a simple shocking; I want to see and feel the characters. But, I just couldn’t. There was an element of emotional depth missing in this. <br><br>I did like the detective aspects. They became quite thrilling. The exploration of the meaning of sanity was the most memorable thing of the novel. It follows the idea that it can be internalised and self-created. In the circumstance this was quite horrendous, but it was also an appropriate reaction. The Victorians didn’t understand what could, and what could not, be inherited from our parents; thus, this character has practically devised their own fate through their pitiful ignorance.<br><br> The stand offs between Robert Audley and Lady Audley were very tense. I was expecting to see something explosive happen after them: <br><br><p>
  <b>
    <i>“You seem to have quite a taste for discussing these horrible subjects," she said, rather scornfully; "you ought to have been a detective police officer."<br><br>"I sometimes think I should have been a good one."<br><br>"Why?"<br><br>"Because I am patient.”</i>
  </b>
</p><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1402059998ra/9888829.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Well he has good reason to be..........(I'm saying no more)<br><br>There was also the separate issue that my university insisted that we read it in periodical segments. We were to read a few chapters each week. I found this quite difficult. I read a lot of different books. So, to have a massive break like that between chapters meant that I’d actually forgotten most of what happened the week before. I had to rely on plot summaries to remind myself of what actually had happened in the book. I seriously recommend avoiding this. Whilst it may be quaint to read it in the same manner of the Victorians, I know for a fact that it hindered my appreciation of the novel. <br><br>I’ve tried to be deliberately cryptic in my review. This book relies on its shock factor, and I didn’t want to give anything away with a plot summary; it would spoil it. This was a thrilling read, the detective elements and the shock factors made it quite exciting, but I think this was mainly the case in the second volume. The third volume was very tense; however, the first volume felt very, very, flat. I did enjoy this, but I have no interest in reading anything else by this author. <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15264537771474373340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1508541093" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1508541093" />
    <a title="Carol She&#39;s So Novel꧁꧂ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25867535-carol-she-s-so-novel"><img alt="Carol She&#39;s So Novel꧁꧂ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575603145p2/25867535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1508541093?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol She&#39;s So Novel꧁꧂ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol She&#39;s So Novel꧁꧂ " href="/user/show/25867535-carol-she-s-so-novel">Carol She&#39;s So Novel꧁꧂ </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/5250019-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Victorian melodrama</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Carol She's So Novel꧁꧂  by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/7323402-carolien">Carolien</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25867535-carol-she-s-so-novel?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25867535-carol-she-s-so-novel?shelf=mystery-murder-crime-suspense">mystery-murder-crime-suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1508541093" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer615455904361148726">This was recommended to me as a cross between Austen & Heyer but other than the abundance of grey eyes (Heyer) this book didn't remind me of either author - more like Conan Doyle or Poe.<br/><br/>Fortunately I <i>love</i> Conan Doyle & Poe.<br/><br/>Fast paced at the start, the book slowed down about three quarters of the way through with a lot of exposition and a lot of melodramatic angst. <i>&</i> for modern tastes, <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ The Hero telling The Villainess his plan of action (& not just once!) was inexplicable.  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide s</a></span></span>
  <span id="freeText615455904361148726" style="display:none">This was recommended to me as a cross between Austen &amp; Heyer but other than the abundance of grey eyes (Heyer) this book didn't remind me of either author - more like Conan Doyle or Poe.<br><br>Fortunately I <i>love</i> Conan Doyle &amp; Poe.<br><br>Fast paced at the start, the book slowed down about three quarters of the way through with a lot of exposition and a lot of melodramatic angst. <i>&amp;</i> for modern tastes, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The Hero telling The Villainess his plan of action (&amp; not just once!) was inexplicable.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> So, as usual I think the book would have been improved with a little pruning.<br><br> I don't think mental illness works quite the way Braddon writes it. However, although Lucy is one of fiction's great evildoers, <br><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://imgur.com/B9oGUFr" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556795292i/27444516.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote><br><br>but I've had to knock half a star off for an ending that <i>really</i> wimped out!<br><br>Edit: Sorry to bump, but I'm cleaning out my Photobucket account &amp; I found the jpg I intended to use for this review (see above) It really is perfect!</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_2149871939" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2149871939" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2149871939?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141198842" href="/book/show/14800164-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=brittish-lit">brittish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2149871939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7416169207304207712"><i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is a Victorian "sensational". My love for "sensational" novels began with <i>The Woman in White</i> by Wilkie Collins. I read that <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is regarded as a rival to <i>The Woman in White</i> and it made me want to read the book. <br/><br/>Told by an unknown narrator, the story is focused on a "secret" of a woman named Lady Audley and the ultimate revelation of what that secret is. When I reflect on the story after reading, it strikes as pretty simple and straight forward. But the beaut</span>
  <span id="freeText7416169207304207712" style="display:none"><i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is a Victorian "sensational". My love for "sensational" novels began with <i>The Woman in White</i> by Wilkie Collins. I read that <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is regarded as a rival to <i>The Woman in White</i> and it made me want to read the book. <br /><br />Told by an unknown narrator, the story is focused on a "secret" of a woman named Lady Audley and the ultimate revelation of what that secret is. When I reflect on the story after reading, it strikes as pretty simple and straight forward. But the beauty lies in the twists and turns Ms. Braddon so carefully employ to keep the reader guessing on the big secret of Lady Audley. She lays down clues and throws hints here and there without giving way too much and keeping the secret well-guarded until the time is right for a confession by Lady Audley. <br /><br />I found the writing of Ms. Braddon remarkable. There is so much beauty in her metaphoric prose. The subtle descriptions of a setting, a character, or anything in general, is so colourful that you are instantly transported to the time and setting of the story. Though her writing is a little verbose at times, this was balanced with the use of simple dialogues. The usage of such techniques made the read both interesting and quick. <br /><br />The set of characters used is unique and intriguing. The main male character, Robert Audley, is a young barrister. He is lazy and purposeless; but by fate, the discovery of Lady Audley's secret is forced on him. The story is both this discovery and Robert's struggle to gather clues and evidence towards this discovery at the risk of his own life. I truly enjoyed reading how Robert's character was developed throughout the story. From an idling and purposeless life, he was slowly but steadily uplifted to a responsible and intellectually mature one. Throughout his journey to unveil the secret, he becomes determined and courageous and he finally understands his duties and responsibilities to his loved ones and the society at large. I was very much impressed with his character development. <br /><br />While Robert Audley represented the detective, judge and the jury, and the executioner, the culprit was Lady Audley, a wicked and treacherous woman who uses her beauty to appease her mercenary intentions. Lady Audley is undoubtedly the most dangerous female villain I have come across. She is resourceful and has a surpassing strength of mind and she can only be compared to a demon in the guise of an angel. The battle between these two main characters that were on opposite ends was truly intriguing. Throughout the read, I was in the middle of a waging war waiting impatiently for the truth and justice to triumph. All the other characters were well placed and developed in the course of the story. They provided a well-structured backdrop for the story. <br /><br />Having read both <i>The Woman in White</i> and <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i>, I now understand why it is said that they rival each other. While <i>The Woman in White</i> touches on the male villainy against innocent and unprotected females, in Lady Audley's Secret the gender role is switched; here we find female villainy against the males. It is said that Ms. Braddon had admired <i>The Woman in White</i> but was not happy with Collin's heroine. And as <i>The Woman in White</i> served as an inspiration for Ms. Braddon to pen Lady Audley's Secret, it is quite natural that Ms. Braddon purposefully used a character who has an incredible strength of mind and who is resourceful as the female lead, though she represented evil. <br /><br />Overall, it was a great "sensational" story and a great reading experience. I loved every minute of reading it. To those who have an appetite for "sensational" stories, I highly recommend <i> Lady Audley's Secret</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7416169207304207712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_177295526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177295526" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/177295526?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">nefarious women and the men who love them</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177295526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16059865445394782326"><b>Top Ten Secrets of Lady Audley</b><br/><br/>10. is a dude<br/>9. is husband's mom<br/>8. killed somebody<br/>7. used to be hooker<br/>6. is screwing the gardener<br/>5. escaped from mental hospital<br/>4. baby is not husband's baby<br/>3. is slowly poisoning husband<br/>2. is dead; husband just having creepy imaginary conversations with ghost <br/>1. Sortof likes Coldplay<br/><br/>The mid 1800s saw the rise of the sensation novel, which brought the spooky atmosphere of the Gothic into normal peoples' homes. Now the spouse (or other family member) was the danger. </span>
  <span id="freeText16059865445394782326" style="display:none"><b>Top Ten Secrets of Lady Audley</b><br /><br />10. is a dude<br />9. is husband's mom<br />8. killed somebody<br />7. used to be hooker<br />6. is screwing the gardener<br />5. escaped from mental hospital<br />4. baby is not husband's baby<br />3. is slowly poisoning husband<br />2. is dead; husband just having creepy imaginary conversations with ghost <br />1. Sortof likes Coldplay<br /><br />The mid 1800s saw the rise of the sensation novel, which brought the spooky atmosphere of the Gothic into normal peoples' homes. Now the spouse (or other family member) was the danger. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1044218550" title="my review" rel="nofollow noopener">Anthony Trollope</a> suggested that they should be "at the same time realistic and sensational...and both in the highest degree." The most famous example is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190831963" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">The Woman in White</a> by Wilkie Collins; the other two pioneers are <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177295906" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">East Lynne</a> by Ellen Wood and this book right here. The genre was immediately successful, and remains so today: if you liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/376140134" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone Girl,</a> here is its grandmother.<br /><br />And sensational it is, chock full of intrigue and reversals and madness. (Victorian madness, with its gleeful disregard for the DSM.) Foreshadowing oozes out its sides like an overstuffed sandwich. Braddon addresses us directly, chatty, sarcastic, sometimes catty, often with what feels a little like misogyny. We're repeatedly reminded of "the horrible things that have been done by women since that day upon which eve was created," and the "feminine prevarication - [the] womanly trickery" ladies are capable of. Hints of sympathy emerge: Lady Audley, she of the secret, knows what "Every school-girl learns sooner or later - I learned that my ultimate fate in life depended upon my marriage." She has no way to better her situation except by marriage, and so she's forced to pursue it by any means necessary. But Braddon is no feminist.<br /><br />The characters are terrific. Phlegmatic Robert Audley drifts his way through the mystery, sighing about all the effort. He will learn seriousness, but not without trial: he'll be forced to smoke innumerable shitty cigars. Bratty Alice Audley (not Lady - her stepdaughter) - "Such a nice girl, too, if she didn't bounce!" - runs away with every scene she's in. Lady Audley herself is a mighty femme fatale. Braddon has a knack for great characters: there are memorable ones in her early detective novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217035166" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood of the Serpent</a> too. She shows an affinity for detective stories in both books.<br /><br />Braddon was very popular in her time, but she's faded now. You wonder why, right? This is a fun book, engaging and well-designed. It lacks something - an authority, a certainty, maybe? And it's not like there's a ton of doubt about how the mystery's going to be resolved. I can't make a super forceful case for re-elevating her. She's good, not great, for me. But I had a good time reading this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16059865445394782326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1379814612" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1379814612" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1379814612?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1379814612" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13346506272705859679">Loved this...great fun. 4.5-stars, rounded down. <br/><br/>As I read Lady Audley's Secret, I kept thinking of Poe, Conan Doyle and Anne Bronte. A nice combination, if I must say so myself. Braddon has created an interesting story line and a creepy environment in which to plunk down her motley set of characters. I loved the conflicting ideas that are present within Lady Audley herself and especially enjoyed the myriad ways she is viewed by the other characters in the story. Her secret did surprise me, and </span>
  <span id="freeText13346506272705859679" style="display:none">Loved this...great fun. 4.5-stars, rounded down. <br /><br />As I read Lady Audley's Secret, I kept thinking of Poe, Conan Doyle and Anne Bronte. A nice combination, if I must say so myself. Braddon has created an interesting story line and a creepy environment in which to plunk down her motley set of characters. I loved the conflicting ideas that are present within Lady Audley herself and especially enjoyed the myriad ways she is viewed by the other characters in the story. Her secret did surprise me, and I confess I thought it would not. <br /><br />Braddon does a wonderful job of creating atmosphere and her descriptive passages are delightful and vivid. She plants red herrings and takes the mind into many dead-end suppositions. She made me laugh a little, but at the same time cringe. In the end, I loved both her story and the way it was delivered. While many things progressed in the novel exactly as I assumed they would, there were a few moments of complete surprise and that is always nice in this genre. I will not hesitate to read more of Braddon's work when I can fit it into my schedule.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13346506272705859679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2992525803" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2992525803" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2992525803?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=london">london</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2992525803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2839346315892250265">Logically, this is not a book that should appeal to me. What makes <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588747.Lady_Audley_s_Secret' title='Lady Audley's Secret by Mary Elizabeth Braddon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lady Audley's Secret</a> the special book that it is? Why do I like it as much as I do? <br/><br/>The book belongs to the genre of Gothic fiction. Here follows a definition:<br/> <br/>“Gothic fiction refers to a style of writing that is characterized by elements of fear, horror, death, and gloom, as well as romantic elements, such as nature, individuality, and very high emotion.”<br/><br/>This fits the book to a T.<br/><br/>When I rate the book, I view it as one of its gen</span>
  <span id="freeText2839346315892250265" style="display:none">Logically, this is not a book that should appeal to me. What makes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588747.Lady_Audley_s_Secret" title="Lady Audley's Secret by Mary Elizabeth Braddon" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Audley's Secret</a> the special book that it is? Why do I like it as much as I do? <br /><br />The book belongs to the genre of Gothic fiction. Here follows a definition:<br /> <br />“Gothic fiction refers to a style of writing that is characterized by elements of fear, horror, death, and gloom, as well as romantic elements, such as nature, individuality, and very high emotion.”<br /><br />This fits the book to a T.<br /><br />When I rate the book, I view it as one of its genre. As such, I deem it wrong to use other criteria when I judge it. The writing style is sentimental, sensational, wordy and over-the-top, but this fits the feel of the tale. It is what it is, and what it does it does well. It exudes atmosphere. It is not supposed to read as a presentation of clear, logical, unbiased fact. The ending <b>is</b> too rosy to be true, but it fits here. I say, if one writes a book of Gothic fiction, lay it on thick, don’t do it halfway.<br /><br />Taste the writing style here:<br /><br />"A glorious old place–a place that visitors fell into raptures with; finding a yearning wish to have done with life and stay there forever, staring into the cool fish-ponds, and counting the bubbles as the roach and carp rose to the surface of the water–a spot in which peace seemed to have taken up her abode, setting her soothing hand on every tree and flower; on the still ponds and quiet alleys."<br /><br />It is in this lovely, idyllic setting that horrible crimes will take place. The contrast increases tension. Readers are shown that appearances are deceptive.<br /><br />“He forgot that love, which is a madness, and a scourge, and a fever, and a delusion, and a snare, is also a mystery, and very imperfectly understood by everyone except the individual sufferer who writhes under its tortures.”<br /><br />With these lines, I hope you sense the tone of the writing.<br /><br />The plot of the story, there being murder and fires, bigamy, child abandonment and insanity, places the story into the genre of sensation novels too. Sensation fiction was a popular literary genre in Britain in the 1860s, which is when it first came out in serial format. The two genres overlap. <br /><br />Thirdly, the author adds an element of psychological intrigue—in relation to both characters and themes. Themes are hinted at, rather than being actually pinned down. For example, feminism and homosexuality are alluded to.<br /><br />“It is because women are never lazy. They don't know what it is to be quiet. They are Semiramides, and Cleopatras, and Joans of Arc, Queen Elizabeths, and Catharines the Second, and they riot in battle, and murder, and clamor in desperation.”<br /><br />In reference to homosexuality, one is kept wondering about the relationship between the barrister Robert Audley and his Eton school friend, George Talboys. Relationships are not explicitly clarified; readers are kept thinking. We are left to analyze the hints given. For example, Robert ends up <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[marrying Clara Talboys, who is George’s sister, and who, coincidentally, has an appearance similar to her brother’s  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />The characters pique a reader’s interest. You come to see why and how they have come to be who they are. Each is different and each believable. What each one must deal with is opened up to the reader. The book has good character portrayals.<br /><br />There is subtle humor written into the lines. Capturing an author’s prose style is difficult. I have tried to explain why I like it. In summary, the prose fits the tale, has humor and keeps you thinking. Even when wordy, it is not unclear. <br /><br />Look at the title. We are to figure out Lady Audley’s secret. After I had read a mere fifteen percent of the novel, I was pretty darn sure who Lady Audley actually was and thus also her secret! Don’t stop reading; she has more secrets than one and other characters are keeping secrets too.<br /><br />I recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588747.Lady_Audley_s_Secret" title="Lady Audley's Secret by Mary Elizabeth Braddon" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Audley's Secret</a> for the month of Halloween. It Is not a simplistic tale of horror. It is very, very good Gothic tale. It is sensational, but it keeps you thinking.<br /><br />Olivia Poulet narrates the audiobook. She reads <u>
  <b>very</b>
</u> quickly. Not always, but often. She also acts the parts rather than simply reading the story. It is, however, not hard to distinguish the words. I have given the narration two stars; I would have preferred a slower tempo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2839346315892250265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olive Fellows (abookolive)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olive Fellows (abookolive)" href="/user/show/17420799-olive-fellows-abookolive">Olive Fellows (abookolive)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141198842" href="/book/show/14800164-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17420799-olive-fellows-abookolive?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3014861268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5024876280605541291">Just like with Agnes Grey, this is one I'd recommend for those looking to get into classics. An approachable sensationalist Victorian novel, this book does raise some interesting questions about the lengths to which we will go to escape poverty. It has intriguing characters and will provide good set up for other classics with a mystery angle (I have Wilkie Collins in mind here). But, as someone with some experience reading Victorian literature, this one failed to intrigue as much as I had hoped.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28186136-lesle?shelf=no-longer-own">no-longer-own</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer14358661872436320945">"Whatever secrets you may have will be secrets forever"<br/><br/>The story starts by describing the Audley manor's garden lush and beautiful. Lady Audley is Lucy who marries an older man, Sir Michael upon his request. Lucy hides and defends her secrets with charm and brutality, she is far from a victim, but unknown to her, another secret exist. <br/><br/>Robert does his best to find clues to what are Lucy's secrets. His sleuthing is to his advantage of what really took place. Fearing her secret is about to be found</span>
  <span id="freeText14358661872436320945" style="display:none">"Whatever secrets you may have will be secrets forever"<br /><br />The story starts by describing the Audley manor's garden lush and beautiful. Lady Audley is Lucy who marries an older man, Sir Michael upon his request. Lucy hides and defends her secrets with charm and brutality, she is far from a victim, but unknown to her, another secret exist. <br /><br />Robert does his best to find clues to what are Lucy's secrets. His sleuthing is to his advantage of what really took place. Fearing her secret is about to be found out, Lucy burns down an Inn with the hope of another outcome. In the end the true secret is revealed. A real diagnosis is found and your left on the edge.<br /><br />Such riveting tale that was actually based on a true incident. The book was hard to put down as there are few dull moments. Braddon's characters are very realistic for the period and has crafted the story well, with what a good read should have: romance, murder, suspense, and wow what a surprising end...oh wait...was it surprising? or forseen?<br /><br />(see comment below)</span>

<div id="review_3208910263" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3208910263" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Luce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Luce" href="/user/show/9429655-anna-luce">Anna Luce</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=2020-reviews">2020-reviews</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3208910263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4176628538332945010">★★★✰✰ 3 stars <br/><br/><i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is a pretty entertaining sensation novel. The story is centred around Lady Audley who, surprise surprise, has a secret. Like most other sensation novels, <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> combines melodrama with an investigation of sorts. Robert Audley, the nephew of Sir Michael, is suspicious of his uncle's new wife, the beautiful and young Lady Audley who, by all accounts, seems to be the embodiment of femininity. After the sudden disappearance of his best friend, Robert </span>
  <span id="freeText4176628538332945010" style="display:none">★★★✰✰ 3 stars <br /><br /><i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> is a pretty entertaining sensation novel. The story is centred around Lady Audley who, surprise surprise, has a secret. Like most other sensation novels, <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> combines melodrama with an investigation of sorts. Robert Audley, the nephew of Sir Michael, is suspicious of his uncle's new wife, the beautiful and young Lady Audley who, by all accounts, seems to be the embodiment of femininity. After the sudden disappearance of his best friend, Robert begins to suspect that Lady Audley's 'delicate flower' front is an act. Throughout the course of the novel he attempts to find evidence to reveal Lady Audley's true nature and identity.<br />There were many amusing passages and the character themselves often struck me as parodies of sorts. Sadly, after Robert realises who Lady Audley is my interest waned. What follows is a series of anticlimactic confrontations. Moreover, Lady Audley was not the 'villainess' I was hoping for. While the way in which she uses her femininity to manipulate others is certainly subversive, ultimately she seems to give up quite easily. Robert's 'hunt' for the truth was far more satisfying that the actual confrontation. <br />Also, I think that the story would have benefited from some more 'vitality' (through humour for example). Maybe readers who haven't read novels by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4012.Wilkie_Collins" title="Wilkie Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilkie Collins</a> will be able to find <i>Lady Audley's Secret</i> to be more absorbing than I did.<br /><br /><a href="https://wishfullyreading.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Read more reviews on my blog</a> / / / <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/9429655-anna-luce" rel="nofollow noopener">View all my reviews on Goodreads</a></span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer2076806238089045283">An entertaining Victorian Era novel that is similar to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5890.The_Woman_in_White' title='The Woman in White by Wilkie Collins' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Woman in White</a>. They both were part of the short lived "Sensation" genre of novels from mid 19th century England, although Lady Audley's Secret doesn't quite measure up to The Woman in White. The beautiful but devious Lady Audley was far and away the most interesting character in the novel. Her nemesis, Robert Audley, nephew to her husband, was so condescending and snobish, that I found myself pulling for the narcissistic, murderous, big</span>
  <span id="freeText2076806238089045283" style="display:none">An entertaining Victorian Era novel that is similar to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5890.The_Woman_in_White" title="The Woman in White by Wilkie Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">The Woman in White</a>. They both were part of the short lived "Sensation" genre of novels from mid 19th century England, although Lady Audley's Secret doesn't quite measure up to The Woman in White. The beautiful but devious Lady Audley was far and away the most interesting character in the novel. Her nemesis, Robert Audley, nephew to her husband, was so condescending and snobish, that I found myself pulling for the narcissistic, murderous, bigamist, Lady Audley.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16921578709101656358">Read for Victober 2017.<br/><br/>Before this month I had never heard of Ms Braddon or her book. Before this year I had never heard the term "sensation novel". What a treat it was to discover! This book didn't really surprise me with the plot twists but it didn't matter because the writing style was very entertaining. The characters were well-developed and intriguing and it was a book that I enjoyed from the first page until the last.</span>

<div id="review_1087305459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1087305459" />
    <a title="Malia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5953660-malia"><img alt="Malia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476920229p2/5953660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1087305459?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malia" href="/user/show/5953660-malia">Malia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5953660-malia?shelf=classic-lit">classic-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1087305459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4468337212909413924">LADY AUDLEY'S SECRET's title would suggest that the book holds in its pages something that would deeply scandalize the reader. I suppose, the fact that it didn't surprise me, when the ominous secret was finally revealed, says something about what our society has grown accustomed to, as well as the fact that I read too many crime novels;-)<br/>That being said, I did rather enjoy reading this book. The story flowed well, and the style of Braddon's writing is very accessible. I read this for a lit cours</span>
  <span id="freeText4468337212909413924" style="display:none">LADY AUDLEY'S SECRET's title would suggest that the book holds in its pages something that would deeply scandalize the reader. I suppose, the fact that it didn't surprise me, when the ominous secret was finally revealed, says something about what our society has grown accustomed to, as well as the fact that I read too many crime novels;-)<br />That being said, I did rather enjoy reading this book. The story flowed well, and the style of Braddon's writing is very accessible. I read this for a lit course, and thus think of it, for the moment at least, in terms of motifs and symbols and feminism and the gothic, but it is actually quite entertaining, too.<br />Despite this, Braddon is far from as famous as her Victorian contemporaries, the Bronte sisters and George Eliot. This is the only book by her that I am familiar with, so I cannot judge what qualities her work may have lacked, which the others possessed. Maybe it was because LADY AUDLEY'S SECRET was considered a sensational novel, and thus not deemed as literary and worthwhile as the other ladies work? Then again, the previously mentioned writers also had one book which was far more famous than their others, so maybe, at the time, Braddon held her own with them... Just thinking. I suppose I'll be reading up on her Wikipedia page;-) Anyway...<br />I have to admit, I do prefer the Brontes, but I think Braddon's novel is far more than a scandalous little volume. It brings up the limited rights of women at the time, and, in a way, gives the reader a dubious answer to what may happen when women are 'forced' to take care of their own best interests. It also highlights facets of Victorian life and traditions, fascinating not simply on a scholarly level.<br />I would recommend reading this, especially if you enjoy other Victorian literature.<br /><br />Find more reviews and bookish fun at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.princessandpen.com">http://www.princessandpen.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4468337212909413924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Brenda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1598974-brenda"><img alt="Brenda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592399991p2/1598974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3439667826?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda" href="/user/show/1598974-brenda">Brenda</a>

  <span id="freeText14365754731506635488" style="display:none">SOLD on what they called during the day “sensation novels”. What a remarkably well crafted story! The characters are so well conceived! Not only am I now interested in other books written during this (Victorian) time period but especially THIS author, what a fan I’ve become! <br /><br />I could liken the unfolding of the “secret” to be a bit like a buffet - we are given so much, in fact, in terms of clues and hints, that I began to feel concerned that perhaps they were spilling all the beans and I, being very alert, was putting 2 and 2 together in a way that would spoil the fun of the story. Clearly I overestimated my own intellect and the author’s prowess. We solve one mystery and there’s another, and another, and another. <br /><br />Highly recommend 4.5 stars for a book that definitely created an admirer of me!</span>

    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447215825p2/48502194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2514342798?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2514342798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9635306398141572595"><i>“What do we know of the mysteries that may hang about the houses we enter?”</i><br/><br/>Murder and madness and mayhem. What fun!<br/><br/>I am not a mystery reader, but this is an excellent mystery. I enjoyed it much more than the classic it is often compared to--The Woman in White. Even though our hero Robert Audley is a barrister, this read as more of a detective than a legal story, and it was more fun finding the clues in this than hearing the lengthy arguments in TWIW.<br/><br/>I’d love to know more about Mary Elizabeth Bra</span>
  <span id="freeText9635306398141572595" style="display:none"><i>“What do we know of the mysteries that may hang about the houses we enter?”</i><br /><br />Murder and madness and mayhem. What fun!<br /><br />I am not a mystery reader, but this is an excellent mystery. I enjoyed it much more than the classic it is often compared to--The Woman in White. Even though our hero Robert Audley is a barrister, this read as more of a detective than a legal story, and it was more fun finding the clues in this than hearing the lengthy arguments in TWIW.<br /><br />I’d love to know more about Mary Elizabeth Braddon. Her characters seemed odd to me at first, but as I read I realized it was their complexity coming through. Was she moralizing? Woman-bashing? Did she condone the resolution of her story? Was she making a point by showing a man’s love and longing for another man? Did she understand how madness may lurk in all of us when she wrote, <i>“Who has not been, or is not to be mad in some lonely hour of life? Who is quite safe from the trembling of the balance?”</i><br /><br />I don’t know, but whatever her motivations, her novel made for “sensational” reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9635306398141572595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2413332154" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2413332154" />
    <a title="Antoinette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette"><img alt="Antoinette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503842818p2/5328952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2413332154?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antoinette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antoinette" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette">Antoinette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199577033" href="/book/show/11700995-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2413332154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13407823276724493993">It's hard to believe this book was written in 1862 as it felt quite modern. When this book came out, it was considered a "sensation" novel. Sensation novels were novels of crime and mystery- novels with an underlying secret. Though this book is titled " Lady Audley's Secret", she is not the only one with a secret.<br/>We meet Lucy Graham, young and beautiful and child-like, who marries Sir Michael Audley, a much older man of good fortune. Sir Michael's nephew, Robert, comes for a visit with his dear </span>
  <span id="freeText13407823276724493993" style="display:none">It's hard to believe this book was written in 1862 as it felt quite modern. When this book came out, it was considered a "sensation" novel. Sensation novels were novels of crime and mystery- novels with an underlying secret. Though this book is titled " Lady Audley's Secret", she is not the only one with a secret.<br />We meet Lucy Graham, young and beautiful and child-like, who marries Sir Michael Audley, a much older man of good fortune. Sir Michael's nephew, Robert, comes for a visit with his dear friend George Tallboys, who subsequently disappears. Where is George? What is Lady Audley hiding?<br />This novel is intricately plotted; it kept me interested throughout and the author sprang a most surprising twist as well.<br />I thoroughly enjoyed this book and would recommend it to anyone who likes a solid classic mystery.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13407823276724493993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/395252670?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie" href="/user/show/2539519-stephanie">Stephanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer395252670" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6214612839489333574">I think this would be a good book either for a teenager looking for "difficult" books or for someone who generally doesn't like literature.<br/><br/>About fifty pages in, I realized this book had absolutely nothing to say about people, or morality, or society. However, reading to the end wasn't a chore, and the last fifty pages or so actually moved at a rapid pace. Good writing on the part of Braddon? Maybe I had just clued in to the fact that half of every page was description that neither served the plo</span>
  <span id="freeText6214612839489333574" style="display:none">I think this would be a good book either for a teenager looking for "difficult" books or for someone who generally doesn't like literature.<br /><br />About fifty pages in, I realized this book had absolutely nothing to say about people, or morality, or society. However, reading to the end wasn't a chore, and the last fifty pages or so actually moved at a rapid pace. Good writing on the part of Braddon? Maybe I had just clued in to the fact that half of every page was description that neither served the plot nor gave symbolic depth ( as in "And now we will discuss furniture as a Metaphor For Life," or "The place was really run down, so I will describe it in hyperbole because hyperbole is clever").<br /><br />Two major problems: The plot of the book often goes absolutely nowhere. Two thirds is Robert Audley wandering around, asking various stock characters about clues for Lucy Audley's past life. Which we already know everything about because of a chapter from her first husband's perspective early on. Pointless. What really makes a difference is how Lucy reacts to the slow disintegration of her plans and the raising of the stakes. But we don't get much of that, at least in the first two thirds of the book.<br /><br />Other major problem: I didn't care about any of the major characters, at least for the reasons you're supposed to care about them. Liked Alycia, really sympathized with her for getting an Evil Stepmother, being estranged from her father, and never being good enough for the man she loves. However, she doesn't do much and pretty much drops out of the picture in the last fifty pages.<br /><br />Robert Audley was a little interesting--as if Bertie Wooster had decided to become a serious detective. Someone who never does anything trying to do something and being completely out of his depth. However, he has no real character traits that make me root for him. As others on this site have pointed out, his friend George is shiftless and inconstant, and his love interest Clara appears for few scenes and is cardboard thin. His uncle's bland too. Robert seems to have some moment of conviction about the need to stop a woman's sin, but it just doesn't take, and feels shoehorned in by the author to make the tale more sensational.<br /><br />Lucy Audley was a character I've seen a million times before--the supposedly ideal woman and wife (older husband) who Uses Her Femininity to get what she wants. I only really became interested when she started to lose it--just seeing how she reacts to all the pressure put on her. <br /><br />I think the story would have worked better if it had been told entirely from Lucy's perspective and just focused on the one character instead of following Robert on his mind-bogglingly obvious interviews. There's a real Macbeth story going on here, where someone does something bad because, what the heck, they really really want to, and thinks "Just this once doesn't make me evil. I won't do anything bad again." But then someone finds out and the person goes "Oh, crap! Why do I have to keep doing more bad things to cover up the first one?"<br /><br />But that's not the story Braddon wanted to tell or her audience wanted to hear. Set up an unnecessarily complicated cast of stock characters, develop none of them because the audience already knows how to pigeon hole them, throw in some long paragraphs of description that add nothing, don't forget your symbolism and literary allusions (they're like easter eggs full of chocolate that the readers just love to pick up, plus they make the author look smarter), and round out the story with some moralistic revelries (oops, reveries) that support societal norms--and you've got a Victorian novel people will be reading for years to come. <br /><br />See Dickens' Tale of Two Cities.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6214612839489333574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
