<div id="review_50309146" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50309146" />
    <a title="Misfit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1375548-misfit"><img alt="Misfit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496595931p2/1375548.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50309146?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Misfit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Misfit" href="/user/show/1375548-misfit">Misfit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50309146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4114005806620949331">Since this is one of those books that to tell too much of the story would ruin it, I'm only giving you the bare bones. Baronet Sir Michael Audley takes himself a young, beautiful (but penniless) wife, but his eighteen year old daughter Alicia is not quite so enthralled with Lucy's charms. Sir Michael's nephew Robert Audley greets his old friend George Talboys on his return from the gold-fields of Australia, but George is anxious to reunite with the wife and child he left behind when he was unabl</span>
  <span id="freeText4114005806620949331" style="display:none">Since this is one of those books that to tell too much of the story would ruin it, I'm only giving you the bare bones. Baronet Sir Michael Audley takes himself a young, beautiful (but penniless) wife, but his eighteen year old daughter Alicia is not quite so enthralled with Lucy's charms. Sir Michael's nephew Robert Audley greets his old friend George Talboys on his return from the gold-fields of Australia, but George is anxious to reunite with the wife and child he left behind when he was unable to support them. An unexpected death notice in a local paper sets George’s world upside down, although a trip with Robert to Audley Court opens up.......<br /><br />Well I'm not telling more than that, I am not into spoilers. This was a highly entertaining and readable mystery - yes you'll guess some of what's going to happen but trust me the author has a red-herring or two and plenty of twists and turns ahead for the reader. Braddon's style was very light and readable, not as heavy handed as some 19C authors can be and I really enjoyed her descriptions of the settings, particularly the very very old Audley Court and its grounds. This book should appeal to mystery fans as well as those looking for something new in 19C lit and perfect for those days when you're looking for something light, albeit with some substance as well. 4/5 stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4114005806620949331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2267861478551828731">This book was really good fun. A 19th century who-dunnit complete with beautiful but cunning villainess, rambling old houses and an upper-class layabout-turned-detective. Fabulous!<br/><br/>This was one of the first "sensation" novels ever written, and while it doesn't have the sophisticated and multi-layered plots of today that keep us guessing until the very end and on the edge of our seets, it is nonetheless a great page turner and so much fun. This book was originally serialised in a paper back in 186</span>
  <span id="freeText2267861478551828731" style="display:none">This book was really good fun. A 19th century who-dunnit complete with beautiful but cunning villainess, rambling old houses and an upper-class layabout-turned-detective. Fabulous!<br /><br />This was one of the first "sensation" novels ever written, and while it doesn't have the sophisticated and multi-layered plots of today that keep us guessing until the very end and on the edge of our seets, it is nonetheless a great page turner and so much fun. This book was originally serialised in a paper back in 1862, and I can imagine eagerly awaiting the next installment as they would have done back then. The language is not complex either, which makes for an easy and much quicker read than some novels of this era.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed <i> Lady Audley's Secret </i> although I am ashamed to say that I had never even heard of the author until I picked this up. I will now be checking out her others.</span>

<div id="review_76759343" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76759343" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76759343?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18937667-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=nineteenth-century">nineteenth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=xx-2017-women">xx-2017-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=women">women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=19thc-read">19thc-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76759343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4927521322477290635">The underlying situation of this book is an older man who loves a young woman who has loved before, and may still love her first love. I quickly made a comparison to Trollope's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1021455.An_Old_Man_s_Love' title='An Old Man's Love by Anthony Trollope' rel='nofollow noopener'>An Old Man's Love</a> which I note was written 20 years <i>after</i> the publication of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588747.Lady_Audley_s_Secret' title='Lady Audley's Secret by Mary Elizabeth Braddon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lady Audley's Secret</a>. Braddon takes her old man/young woman in an entirely different direction - so different that, after the opening pages, I completely disregarded the comparison.<br/><br/>Some GR readers have this shelved as gothic. To me, it falls some</span>
  <span id="freeText4927521322477290635" style="display:none">The underlying situation of this book is an older man who loves a young woman who has loved before, and may still love her first love. I quickly made a comparison to Trollope's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1021455.An_Old_Man_s_Love" title="An Old Man's Love by Anthony Trollope" rel="nofollow noopener">An Old Man's Love</a> which I note was written 20 years <i>after</i> the publication of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/588747.Lady_Audley_s_Secret" title="Lady Audley's Secret by Mary Elizabeth Braddon" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Audley's Secret</a>. Braddon takes her old man/young woman in an entirely different direction - so different that, after the opening pages, I completely disregarded the comparison.<br /><br />Some GR readers have this shelved as gothic. To me, it falls somewhere between psychological fiction and gothic, sometimes more one than the other but never quite fitting either. There were brief passages where those critical of the flowery phrases of 19th Century fiction could be justified. Even I, who love 19th Century fiction, rolled my eyes on occasion. While it in no way ever resembles the spare and simplified prose which has become popular, this is quite readable. <br /><br />I was pleasantly surprised to find some quite decent characterization in what I expected to be simply a plot-driven novel. It is told mostly in third person ominipotent, but we spend a lot of time with one character, Robert Audley, and in that part we know little of what is going on with the other characters. Robert grows from who we think of as a man who lives only for his self-interest and a man with no purpose in life, to one with a single-mindedness of purpose and who looks out for the interest of others. And then there is this:<blockquote>"...Why don't I love her? Why is it that although I know her to be pretty, and pure, and good, and truthful, I don't love her? Her image never haunts me, except reproachfully. I never see her in my dreams. I never wake up suddenly in the dead of the night with her eyes shining upon me and her warm breath upon my cheek, or with the fingers of her soft hand clinging to mine. No, I'm not in love with her, I can't fall in love with her."</blockquote>This, I believe, is Braddon's most well-known book, and it may be her best. I am more than willing to try others and see if that is correct. After about 50-60 pages, I couldn't wait to find time to put my nose in this book. That would usually be a 5-star read for me, but for some reason I'm not quite there. However, it is certainly at the very top of my 4-star reads. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4927521322477290635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1665929895" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1665929895" />
    <a title="Alysia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10456446-alysia"><img alt="Alysia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454768903p2/10456446.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1665929895?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alysia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alysia" href="/user/show/10456446-alysia">Alysia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141198842" href="/book/show/14800164-lady-audley-s-secret">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1665929895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18213296401331105981">More like a solid 3.5* <br/><br/>Some long winded parts on the treachery of women irked me. Otherwise very enjoyable. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5885283639613984937">Things are not always as they appear, if there's a lesson to be learned from this book...there it is in a nut shell. There was a major curve ball thrown at the end and I was pleasantly surprised. I guess this is what was considered "chick-lit" in the 1800s. Bigamy, murder, lunacy, etc. Good deal!</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sXEW6AimbIWDqR1WadcYBWW8JZpa6sdweglPLoeKvYz/kDCDzYdyGnFm+LhDj0C5cOW2Ruhq4iiTFZxP67jq4g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('c2yzZ+qar+X/1JcQ8wYGuxZ8u6XaBrQjoAMTM+mCmKo9jZUML7uxeg0bcv7ZXl4HAyUoeWiGkXtJH8BShbDPxA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Unlike the &quot;New Woman&quot; novels of the era which challenged the idea that the unmarried woman was superfluous, Gissing satirizes that image and portrays women as &quot;odd&quot; and marginal in relation to an ideal. Set in a grimy, fog-ridden London, Gissing\'s &quot;odd&quot; women range from the idealistic, financially self-sufficient Mary Barfoot to the Madden sisters who struggle to subsist in low paying jobs and little chance for joy. With narrative detachment, Gissing portrays contemporary society\'s blatant ambivalence towards its own period of transition. Judged by contemporary critics to be as provocative as Zola and Ibsen, Gissing produced an &quot;intensely modern&quot; work as the issues it raises remain the subject of contemporary //<![CDATA[

One is the shy Caroline Helstone, who is trapped in the oppressive atmosphere of a Yorkshire rectory and whose bare life symbolizes the plight of single women in the nineteenth century. The other is the vivacious Shirley Keeldar, who inherits a local estate and whose wealth liberates her from convention.A work that combines social commentary with the more private preoccupations of Jane Eyre, Shirley demonstrates the full range of Brontë\'s literary talent. &quot;Shirley is a revolutionary novel,&quot; wrote Brontë biographer Lyndall Gordon. &quot;Shirley follows Jane Eyre as a new exemplar but so much a forerunner of the feminist of the later twentieth century that it is hard to believe in her actual existence in 1811-12. She is a theoretic possibility: what a woman might be if she combined independence and means of her own with intellect. Charlotte Brontë imagined a new form of power, equal to that of men, in a confident young woman [whose] extraordinary freedom has accustomed her to think for herself....Shirley [is] Brontë\'s most Her narrator, the autobiogr<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1648667975838640933\" style=\"display:none\">With her final novel, Villette, Charlotte Brontë reached the height of her artistic power. First published in 1853, Villette is Brontë\'s most accomplished and deeply felt work, eclipsing even Jane Eyre in critical acclaim. Her narrator, the autobiographical Lucy Snowe, flees England and a tragic past to become an instructor in a French boarding school in the town of Villette. There she unexpectedly confronts her feelings of love and longing as she witnesses the fitful romance between Dr. John, a handsome young Englishman, and Ginerva Fanshawe, a beautiful coquette. The first pain brings others, and with them comes the heartache Lucy has tried so long to escape.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/45896.Mary_Elizabeth_Braddon">About Mary Elizabeth Braddon</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17198375444836166350">Mary Elizabeth Braddon was a British Victorian era popular novelist. She was an extremely prolific writer, producing some 75 novels with very inventive plots. The most famous one is her first novel, <b>Lady Audley's Secret</b> (1862), which won her recognition and fortune as well. The novel has been in print ever since, and has been dramatised and filmed several times.<br/><br/>Braddon also founded <i>Belgravia Magaz</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17198375444836166350" style="display:none">Mary Elizabeth Braddon was a British Victorian era popular novelist. She was an extremely prolific writer, producing some 75 novels with very inventive plots. The most famous one is her first novel, <b>Lady Audley's Secret</b> (1862), which won her recognition and fortune as well. The novel has been in print ever since, and has been dramatised and filmed several times.<br /><br />Braddon also founded <i>Belgravia Magazine</i> (1866), which presented readers with serialized sensation novels, poems, travel narratives, and biographies, as well as essays on fashion, history, science. She also edited <i>Temple Bar Magazine</i>. Braddon's legacy is tied to the Sensation Fiction of the 1860s.<br /><br />She is also the mother of novelist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4048730.W_B__Maxwell" title="W.B. Maxwell" rel="nofollow noopener">W.B. Maxwell</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17198375444836166350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
